	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Al-Andalus</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">The territories of the Iberian Peninsula under Moorish rule between 711 and 1492</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the historical region. For the modern-day region, see <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>. For the area in Kuwait, see <a href="/wiki/Al_Andalus,_Kuwait" title="Al Andalus, Kuwait">Al Andalus, Kuwait</a>. For the musical group, see <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus_Ensemble" title="Al-Andalus Ensemble">Al-Andalus Ensemble</a>.</div>

</p>

<p><b>Al-Andalus</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> also known as <b>Muslim Iberia</b>, or <b>Islamic Iberia</b>, was a <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> Muslim territory and cultural domain that in its early period included most of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberia</a>. At its greatest geographical extent, it occupied the northwest of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> and a part of present-day southern France, <a href="/wiki/Septimania" title="Septimania">Septimania</a> (8th century), and for nearly a century (9th–10th centuries) extended its control from <a href="/wiki/Fraxinet" class="mw-redirect" title="Fraxinet">Fraxinet</a> over the Alpine passes which connect Italy with the remainder of Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  The name more generally describes the parts of the peninsula governed by <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> (given the generic name of <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>) at various times between 711 and 1492, though the boundaries  changed constantly as the Christian <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a> progressed,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ruano(Spain)2002_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruano(Spain)2002-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> eventually shrinking to the south and finally to the vassalage of the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">Emirate of Granada</a>.
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">Umayyad conquest of Hispania</a>, al-Andalus, then at its greatest extent, was divided into five administrative units, corresponding roughly to modern <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castile_(historical_region)" title="Castile (historical region)">Castile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">León</a>, <a href="/wiki/Navarre" title="Navarre">Navarre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">Aragon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/County_of_Barcelona" title="County of Barcelona">County of Barcelona</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Septimania" title="Septimania">Septimania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> As a political domain, it successively constituted a province of the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>, initiated by the Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Walid_I" title="Al-Walid I">Al-Walid I</a> (711–750); the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Emirate of Córdoba">Emirate of Córdoba</a> (c. 750–929); the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Caliphate of Córdoba">Caliphate of Córdoba</a> (929–1031); and the Caliphate of Córdoba's <i><a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">taifa</a></i> (successor) kingdoms. Rule under these kingdoms led to a rise in cultural exchange and cooperation between Muslims and <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>. <a href="/wiki/People_of_the_Book" title="People of the Book">Christians and Jews</a> were subject to a special tax called <a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">Jizya</a>, to the state, which in return provided internal autonomy in practicing their religion and offered the same level of protections by the Muslim rulers. The jizya was not only a tax, however, but also a symbolic expression of subordination.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the Caliphate of Córdoba, al-Andalus was a beacon of learning, and the city of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Andalusia" class="mw-redirect" title="Córdoba, Andalusia">Córdoba</a>, the largest in Europe, became one of the leading cultural and economic centres throughout the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>, Europe, and the Islamic world. Achievements that advanced Islamic and Western science came from al-Andalus, including major advances in trigonometry (<a href="/wiki/Jabir_ibn_Aflah" title="Jabir ibn Aflah">Geber</a>), astronomy (<a href="/wiki/Arzachel" class="mw-redirect" title="Arzachel">Arzachel</a>), surgery (<a href="/wiki/Abulcasis" class="mw-redirect" title="Abulcasis">Abulcasis</a>), pharmacology (<a href="/wiki/Avenzoar" class="mw-redirect" title="Avenzoar">Avenzoar</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-rediscScience_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rediscScience-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">agronomy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ibn_Bassal" title="Ibn Bassal">Ibn Bassal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_l-Khayr_al-Ishb%C4%ABl%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Abū l-Khayr al-Ishbīlī">Abū l-Khayr al-Ishbīlī</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Zaimeche_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zaimeche-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and other fields. Al-Andalus became a major educational center for Europe and the lands around the Mediterranean Sea as well as a conduit for cultural and scientific exchange between the Islamic and Christian worlds.<sup id="cite_ref-rediscScience_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rediscScience-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For much of its history, al-Andalus existed in conflict with Christian kingdoms to the north. After the fall of the Umayyad caliphate, al-Andalus was fragmented into minor states and principalities. Attacks from the Christians intensified, led by the Castilians under <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_VI_of_Le%C3%B3n_and_Castile" title="Alfonso VI of León and Castile">Alfonso VI</a>. The Almoravid empire intervened and repelled the Christian attacks on the region, deposing the weak Andalusi Muslim princes and included al-Andalus under direct <a href="/wiki/Berber_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Berber people">Berber</a> rule. In the next century and a half, al-Andalus became a province of the Berber Muslim empires of the <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Almohads" class="mw-redirect" title="Almohads">Almohads</a>, both based in <a href="/wiki/Marrakesh" title="Marrakesh">Marrakesh</a>.
</p><p>Ultimately, the Christian kingdoms in the north of the Iberian Peninsula overpowered the Muslim states to the south. In 1085, Alfonso VI captured <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Spain" title="Toledo, Spain">Toledo</a>, starting a gradual decline of Muslim power. With the fall of Córdoba in 1236, most of the south quickly fell under Christian rule and the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">Emirate of Granada</a> became a tributary state of the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Kingdom of Castile</a> two years later. In 1249, the Portuguese Reconquista culminated with the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Algarve" title="Algarve">Algarve</a> by <a href="/wiki/Afonso_III_of_Portugal" title="Afonso III of Portugal">Afonso III</a>, leaving Granada as the last Muslim state on the Iberian Peninsula. Finally, on January 2, 1492,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Emir <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_XII_of_Granada" title="Muhammad XII of Granada">Muhammad XII</a> surrendered the Emirate of Granada to Queen <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella I of Castile</a>, completing the Christian Reconquista of the peninsula.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Andalusia" title="Name of Andalusia">Name of Andalusia</a></div>
<p>The toponym <i>al-Andalus</i> is first attested by inscriptions on coins minted in 716 by the new Muslim government of Iberia.<sup id="cite_ref-PanzramCallegarin2018_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PanzramCallegarin2018-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> These coins, called <i><a href="/wiki/Dinars" class="mw-redirect" title="Dinars">dinars</a></i>, were inscribed in both <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MMOA_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MMOA-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glick2005_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glick2005-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The etymology of the name "<i>al-Andalus</i>" has traditionally been derived from the name of the <i><a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a></i>; however, proposals since the 1980s have challenged this tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In 1986, Joaquín Vallvé proposed that "<i>al-Andalus</i>" was a corruption of the name <i><a href="/wiki/Atlantis" title="Atlantis">Atlantis</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Vallvé1986_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vallvé1986-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Halm in 1989 derived the name from a Gothic term, <i>*landahlauts</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Halm1989_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halm1989-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and in 2002, Georg Bossong suggested its derivation from a pre-Roman substrate.<sup id="cite_ref-Bossong2002_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bossong2002-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Province_of_the_Umayyad_Caliphate">Province of the Umayyad Caliphate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Province of the Umayyad Caliphate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">Umayyad conquest of Hispania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/220px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="101" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/330px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/440px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="413" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Age of the <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">Caliphs</a> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #a1584e; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, 622–632</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ef9070; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>, 632–661</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fad07d; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>, 661–750</div></div></div></div>
<p>During the caliphate of the Umayyad Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Walid_I" title="Al-Walid I">Al-Walid I</a>, the commander <a href="/wiki/Tariq_ibn-Ziyad" class="mw-redirect" title="Tariq ibn-Ziyad">Tariq ibn-Ziyad</a> led a small force that landed at <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> on April 30, 711, ostensibly to intervene in a <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigothic</a> civil war. After a decisive victory over King <a href="/wiki/Roderic" title="Roderic">Roderic</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Guadalete" title="Battle of Guadalete">Battle of Guadalete</a> on July 19, 711, Tariq ibn-Ziyad, joined by Arab governor <a href="/wiki/Musa_ibn_Nusayr" title="Musa ibn Nusayr">Musa ibn Nusayr</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ifriqiya" title="Ifriqiya">Ifriqiya</a>, brought most of the <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Kingdom" title="Visigothic Kingdom">Visigothic Kingdom</a> under Muslim occupation in a seven-year campaign. They crossed the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a> and occupied Visigothic <a href="/wiki/Septimania" title="Septimania">Septimania</a> in southern France.
</p><p>Most of the Iberian peninsula became part of the expanding <a href="/wiki/Umayyad" class="mw-redirect" title="Umayyad">Umayyad Empire</a>, under the name of <i>al-Andalus</i>. It was organized as a province subordinate to <a href="/wiki/Ifriqiya" title="Ifriqiya">Ifriqiya</a>, so, for the first few decades, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Umayyad_Governors_of_Al-Andalus" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Umayyad Governors of Al-Andalus">governors of al-Andalus</a> were appointed by the emir of <a href="/wiki/Kairouan" title="Kairouan">Kairouan</a>, rather than the Caliph in Damascus. The regional capital was set at <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Andalusia" class="mw-redirect" title="Córdoba, Andalusia">Córdoba</a>, and the first influx of Muslim settlers was widely distributed.
</p><p>The small army Tariq led in the initial conquest consisted mostly of Berbers, while Musa's largely Arab force of over 12,000 soldiers was accompanied by a group of <a href="/wiki/Mawla" title="Mawla"><i>mawālī</i></a> (Arabic, موالي), that is, non-Arab Muslims, who were clients of the Arabs. The Berber soldiers accompanying Tariq were garrisoned in the centre and the north of the peninsula, as well as in the Pyrenees,<sup id="cite_ref-Collins2012_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins2012-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Berber_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Berber people">Berber</a> colonists who followed settled in all parts of the country – north, east, south and west.<sup id="cite_ref-Ṭāha2016_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ṭāha2016-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Visigothic lords who agreed to recognize Muslim suzerainty were allowed to retain their fiefs (notably, in Murcia, Galicia, and the Ebro valley). Resistant Visigoths took refuge in the <a href="/wiki/Cantabria" title="Cantabria">Cantabrian</a> highlands, where they carved out a rump state, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">Kingdom of Asturias</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Iberian_Peninsula_750-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Map_Iberian_Peninsula_750-en.svg/220px-Map_Iberian_Peninsula_750-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Map_Iberian_Peninsula_750-en.svg/330px-Map_Iberian_Peninsula_750-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Map_Iberian_Peninsula_750-en.svg/440px-Map_Iberian_Peninsula_750-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="827" data-file-height="574" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Iberian_Peninsula_750-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The province of al-Andalus in 750</div></div></div>
<p>In the 720s, the al-Andalus governors launched several <i>sa'ifa</i> raids into <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">Aquitaine</a>, but were severely defeated by Duke <a href="/wiki/Odo_the_Great" title="Odo the Great">Odo the Great</a> of Aquitaine at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Toulouse_(721)" title="Battle of Toulouse (721)">Battle of Toulouse (721)</a>. However, after crushing Odo's Berber ally <a href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_Naissa" class="mw-redirect" title="Uthman ibn Naissa">Uthman ibn Naissa</a> on the eastern Pyrenees, <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rahman_Al_Ghafiqi" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdul Rahman Al Ghafiqi">Abdul Rahman Al Ghafiqi</a> led an expedition north across the western Pyrenees and defeated the Aquitanian duke, who in turn appealed to the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Frankish</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a> for assistance, offering to place himself under Carolingian sovereignty. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tours" title="Battle of Tours">Battle of Poitiers</a> in 732, the al-Andalus raiding army was defeated by Charles Martel. In 734, the Andalusi launched raids to the east, capturing <a href="/wiki/Avignon" title="Avignon">Avignon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arles" title="Arles">Arles</a> and overran much of <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a>. In 737, they traveled up the <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="Rhône">Rhône</a> valley, reaching as far north as <a href="/wiki/Burgundy_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgundy (region)">Burgundy</a>. Charles Martel of the Franks, with the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Liutprand,_King_of_the_Lombards" title="Liutprand, King of the Lombards">Liutprand</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a>, invaded Burgundy and Provence and expelled the raiders by 739.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mezquita-Catedral_de_Cordoba_01.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mezquita-Catedral_de_Cordoba_01.JPG/170px-Mezquita-Catedral_de_Cordoba_01.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="308" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mezquita-Catedral_de_Cordoba_01.JPG/255px-Mezquita-Catedral_de_Cordoba_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mezquita-Catedral_de_Cordoba_01.JPG/340px-Mezquita-Catedral_de_Cordoba_01.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1131" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mezquita-Catedral_de_Cordoba_01.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interior of the <a href="/wiki/Mosque%E2%80%93Cathedral_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Mosque–Cathedral of Córdoba">Mosque–Cathedral of Córdoba</a> formerly the Great Mosque of Córdoba. The original mosque (742), since much enlarged, was built on the site of the Visigothic Christian 'Saint Vincent basilica' (600).</div></div></div>
<p>Relations between Arabs and <a href="/wiki/Berber_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Berber people">Berbers</a> in al-Andalus had been tense in the years after the conquest. Berbers heavily outnumbered the Arabs in the province, had done the bulk of the fighting, and were assigned the harsher duties (e.g. garrisoning the more troubled areas). Although some Arab governors had cultivated their Berber lieutenants, others had grievously mistreated them. Mutinies by Berber soldiers were frequent; e.g., in 729, the Berber commander Munnus had revolted and managed to carve out a rebel state in <a href="/wiki/Cerdanya" title="Cerdanya">Cerdanya</a> for a while.
</p><p>In 740, a <a href="/wiki/Berber_Revolt" title="Berber Revolt">Berber Revolt</a> erupted in the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> (North Africa). To put down the rebellion, the Umayyad Caliph <a href="/wiki/Hisham_ibn_Abd_al-Malik" title="Hisham ibn Abd al-Malik">Hisham</a> dispatched a large Arab army, composed of regiments (<i><a href="/wiki/Jund" title="Jund">Junds</a></i>) of <a href="/wiki/Bilad_Ash-Sham" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilad Ash-Sham">Bilad Ash-Sham</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> to North Africa. But the great Umayyad army was crushed by the Berber rebels at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bagdoura" title="Battle of Bagdoura">Battle of Bagdoura</a> (in Morocco). Heartened by the victories of their North African brethren, the Berbers of al-Andalus quickly raised their own revolt. Berber garrisons in the north of the Iberian Peninsula mutinied, deposed their Arab commanders, and organized a large rebel army to march against the strongholds of Toledo, Cordoba, and Algeciras.
</p><p>In 741, Balj b. Bishr led a detachment of some 10,000 Arab troops across the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">straits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Collins201212_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins201212-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The Arab governor of al-Andalus, joined by this force, crushed the Berber rebels in a series of ferocious battles in 742. However, a quarrel immediately erupted between the Syrian commanders and the Andalusi, the so-called "original Arabs" of the earlier contingents. The Syrians defeated them at the hard-fought Battle of Aqua Portora in August 742 but were too few to impose themselves on the province.
</p><p>The quarrel was settled in 743 when <a href="/wiki/Abu_l-Hattar_al_Husam_ibn_Darar_al-Kalbi" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu l-Hattar al Husam ibn Darar al-Kalbi">Abū l-Khaṭṭār al-Ḥusām</a>, the new governor of al-Andalus, assigned the Syrians to regimental fiefs across al-Andalus<sup id="cite_ref-Makki1992_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makki1992-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> – the Damascus jund was established in Elvira (<a href="/wiki/Granada" title="Granada">Granada</a>), the Jordan jund in Rayyu (<a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1laga" title="Málaga">Málaga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archidona" title="Archidona">Archidona</a>), the Jund Filastin in <a href="/wiki/Medina-Sidonia" title="Medina-Sidonia">Medina-Sidonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerez" class="mw-redirect" title="Jerez">Jerez</a>, the Emesa (Hims) jund in <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niebla,_Spain" title="Niebla, Spain">Niebla</a>, and the Qinnasrin jund in <a href="/wiki/Ja%C3%A9n,_Spain" title="Jaén, Spain">Jaén</a>. The Egypt jund was divided between <a href="/wiki/Beja_(Portugal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Beja (Portugal)">Beja</a> (<a href="/wiki/Alentejo" title="Alentejo">Alentejo</a>) in the west and Tudmir (<a href="/wiki/Murcia" title="Murcia">Murcia</a>) in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The arrival of the Syrians substantially increased the Arab element in the Iberian peninsula and helped strengthen the Muslim hold on the south. However, at the same time, unwilling to be governed, the Syrian <i>junds</i> carried on an existence of autonomous feudal anarchy, severely destabilizing the authority of the governor of al-Andalus.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abdul_al_Rahman_I.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Abdul_al_Rahman_I.jpg/170px-Abdul_al_Rahman_I.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Abdul_al_Rahman_I.jpg/255px-Abdul_al_Rahman_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Abdul_al_Rahman_I.jpg/340px-Abdul_al_Rahman_I.jpg 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="558" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abdul_al_Rahman_I.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_I" title="Abd al-Rahman I">Abd al-Rahman I</a></div></div></div>
<p>A second significant consequence of the revolt was the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Asturias" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of the Asturias">Kingdom of the Asturias</a>, hitherto confined to enclaves in the Cantabrian highlands. After the rebellious Berber garrisons evacuated the northern frontier fortresses, the Christian king <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_I_of_Asturias" title="Alfonso I of Asturias">Alfonso I of Asturias</a> set about immediately seizing the empty forts for himself, quickly adding the northwestern provinces of <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n_(province)" class="mw-redirect" title="León (province)">León</a> to his fledgling kingdom. The Asturians evacuated the Christian populations from the towns and villages of the Galician-Leonese lowlands, creating an empty buffer zone in the <a href="/wiki/Douro_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Douro River">Douro River</a> valley (the "<a href="/wiki/Desert_of_the_Duero" class="mw-redirect" title="Desert of the Duero">Desert of the Duero</a>"). This newly emptied frontier remained roughly in place for the next few centuries as the boundary between the Christian north and the Islamic south. Between this frontier and its heartland in the south, the al-Andalus state had three large <a href="/wiki/March_(territory)" title="March (territory)">march territories</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Thughur" class="mw-redirect" title="Thughur">thughur</a></i>): the <a href="/wiki/Lower_March" title="Lower March">Lower March</a> (capital initially at <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9rida,_Spain" title="Mérida, Spain">Mérida</a>, later <a href="/wiki/Badajoz" title="Badajoz">Badajoz</a>), the Middle March (centered at Toledo), and the <a href="/wiki/Upper_March" title="Upper March">Upper March</a> (centered at <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza" title="Zaragoza">Zaragoza</a>).
</p><p>These disturbances and disorders also allowed the Franks, now under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a>, to invade the strategic strip of <a href="/wiki/Septimania" title="Septimania">Septimania</a> in 752, hoping to deprive al-Andalus of an easy launching pad for raids into <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a>. After a lengthy siege, the last Arab stronghold, the citadel of <a href="/wiki/Narbonne" title="Narbonne">Narbonne</a>, finally <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Narbonne_(752%E2%80%93759)" class="mw-redirect" title="Siege of Narbonne (752–759)">fell to the Franks in 759</a>. Al-Andalus was sealed off at the Pyrenees.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The third consequence of the Berber revolt was the collapse of the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> Caliphate over the western provinces. With the Umayyad Caliphs distracted by the challenge of the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid">Abbasids</a> in the east, the western provinces of the Maghreb and al-Andalus spun out of their control. From around 745, the <a href="/wiki/Fihrids" title="Fihrids">Fihrids</a>, an illustrious local Arab clan descended from <a href="/wiki/Uqba_ibn_Nafi" title="Uqba ibn Nafi">Oqba ibn Nafi al-Fihri</a>, seized power in the western provinces and ruled them almost as a private family empire of their own – <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_ibn_Habib_al-Fihri" title="Abd al-Rahman ibn Habib al-Fihri">Abd al-Rahman ibn Habib al-Fihri</a> in Ifriqiya and <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_ibn_%27Abd_al-Rahman_al-Fihri" title="Yusuf ibn &#39;Abd al-Rahman al-Fihri">Yūsuf al-Fihri</a> in al-Andalus. The Fihrids welcomed the fall of the Umayyads in the east, in 750, and sought to reach an understanding with the Abbasids, hoping they might be allowed to continue their autonomous existence.  But when the Abbasids rejected the offer and demanded submission, the Fihrids declared independence and, probably out of spite, invited the deposed remnants of the Umayyad clan to take refuge in their dominions. It was a fateful decision that they soon regretted, for the Umayyads, the sons and grandsons of caliphs, had a more legitimate claim to rule than the Fihrids themselves. Rebellious-minded local lords, disenchanted with the autocratic rule of the Fihrids, conspired with the arriving Umayyad exiles.
</p>
<h3><span id="Umayyad_Emirate_and_Caliphate_of_C.C3.B3rdoba"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Umayyad_Emirate_and_Caliphate_of_Córdoba">Umayyad Emirate and Caliphate of Córdoba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Umayyad Emirate and Caliphate of Córdoba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Emirate of Córdoba">Emirate of Córdoba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caliphate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Caliphate of Córdoba">Caliphate of Córdoba</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:La_civilitzaci%C3%B3_del_califat_de_C%C3%B2rdova_en_temps_d%27Abd-al-Rahman_III.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/La_civilitzaci%C3%B3_del_califat_de_C%C3%B2rdova_en_temps_d%27Abd-al-Rahman_III.jpg/220px-La_civilitzaci%C3%B3_del_califat_de_C%C3%B2rdova_en_temps_d%27Abd-al-Rahman_III.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/La_civilitzaci%C3%B3_del_califat_de_C%C3%B2rdova_en_temps_d%27Abd-al-Rahman_III.jpg/330px-La_civilitzaci%C3%B3_del_califat_de_C%C3%B2rdova_en_temps_d%27Abd-al-Rahman_III.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/La_civilitzaci%C3%B3_del_califat_de_C%C3%B2rdova_en_temps_d%27Abd-al-Rahman_III.jpg/440px-La_civilitzaci%C3%B3_del_califat_de_C%C3%B2rdova_en_temps_d%27Abd-al-Rahman_III.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3052" data-file-height="1960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:La_civilitzaci%C3%B3_del_califat_de_C%C3%B2rdova_en_temps_d%27Abd-al-Rahman_III.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Abd-ar-Rahman_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Abd-ar-Rahman III">Abd-ar-Rahman III</a> and his court receiving an ambassador in <a href="/wiki/Medina_Azahara" title="Medina Azahara">Medina Azahara</a>, Còrdoba</div></div></div>
<p>In 756, the exiled Umayyad prince <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_I" title="Abd al-Rahman I">Abd al-Rahman I</a> (nicknamed <i>al-Dākhil</i>, the 'Immigrant') ousted Yūsuf al-Fihri to establish himself as the <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">Emir</a> of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a>. He refused to submit to the Abbasid caliph, as Abbasid forces had killed most of his family. Over a thirty-year reign, he established a tenuous rule over much of al-Andalus, overcoming partisans of both the al-Fihri family and of the Abbasid caliph.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the next century and a half, his descendants continued as emirs of Córdoba with nominal control over the rest of al-Andalus and sometimes parts of western <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, but with real control, particularly over the marches along the Christian border, vacillating depending on the competence of the individual emir. Indeed, the power of emir <a href="/wiki/Abdallah_ibn_Muhammad" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdallah ibn Muhammad">Abdallah ibn Muhammad</a> (circa 900) did not extend beyond Córdoba itself. But his grandson <a href="/wiki/Abd-al-Rahman_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Abd-al-Rahman III">Abd-al-Rahman III</a>, who succeeded him in 912, not only rapidly restored Umayyad power throughout al-Andalus but extended it into western North Africa as well. In 929 he proclaimed himself <a href="/wiki/Caliph_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph of Córdoba">Caliph</a>, elevating the emirate to a position competing in prestige not only with the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> caliph in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> but also the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid</a> caliph in <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a> – with whom he was competing for control of North Africa.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_Andalus_%26_Christian_Kingdoms.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Al_Andalus_%26_Christian_Kingdoms.png/220px-Al_Andalus_%26_Christian_Kingdoms.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Al_Andalus_%26_Christian_Kingdoms.png/330px-Al_Andalus_%26_Christian_Kingdoms.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Al_Andalus_%26_Christian_Kingdoms.png/440px-Al_Andalus_%26_Christian_Kingdoms.png 2x" data-file-width="670" data-file-height="497" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_Andalus_%26_Christian_Kingdoms.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Caliphate of Cordoba in the early 10th century</div></div></div>
<p>The period of the Caliphate is seen as the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">golden age</a> of al-Andalus. Crops produced using irrigation, along with food imported from the Middle East, provided the area around Córdoba and some other <i>Andalusī</i> cities with an agricultural economic sector that was the most advanced in Europe by far, sparking the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zaimeche_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zaimeche-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Squatriti_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squatriti-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Among European cities, Córdoba under the Caliphate, with a population of perhaps 500,000, eventually overtook <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> as the largest and most prosperous city in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Chandler_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chandler-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Within the Islamic world, Córdoba was one of the leading cultural centres. The work of its most important philosophers and scientists (notably <a href="/wiki/Abu_al-Qasim_al-Zahrawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu al-Qasim al-Zahrawi">Abulcasis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>) had a major influence on the intellectual life of medieval Europe.
</p><p>Muslims and non-Muslims often came from abroad to study in the famous libraries and universities of al-Andalus, mainly after the reconquest of Toledo in 1085 and the establishment of translation institutions such as the <a href="/wiki/Toledo_School_of_Translators" title="Toledo School of Translators">Toledo School of Translators</a>. The most noted of those was <a href="/wiki/Michael_Scot" title="Michael Scot">Michael Scot</a> (c. 1175 to c. 1235), who took the works of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Rushd" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Rushd">Ibn Rushd</a> ("Averroes") and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Sina" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Sina">Ibn Sina</a> ("Avicenna") to Italy. This transmission of ideas remains one of the greatest in history,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Puffery" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="Unverified descriptions (September 2019)">peacock&#160;term</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> significantly affecting the formation of the European <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MP_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MP-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taifas_period"><i>Taifas</i> period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Taifas period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">Taifa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taifas2.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Taifas2.gif/220px-Taifas2.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Taifas2.gif/330px-Taifas2.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Taifas2.gif/440px-Taifas2.gif 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="779" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taifas2.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The taifas (green) in 1031 AD</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hisham_II_of_C%C3%B3rdoba_Dinar_94227.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Hisham_II_of_C%C3%B3rdoba_Dinar_94227.jpg/220px-Hisham_II_of_C%C3%B3rdoba_Dinar_94227.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="106" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/85/Hisham_II_of_C%C3%B3rdoba_Dinar_94227.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="325" data-file-height="157" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hisham_II_of_C%C3%B3rdoba_Dinar_94227.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold dinar minted in Córdoba during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Hisham_II" title="Hisham II">Hisham II</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Caliphate of Córdoba effectively collapsed during a <a href="/wiki/Fitna_of_al-Andalus" title="Fitna of al-Andalus">ruinous civil war</a> between 1009 and 1013, although it was not finally abolished until 1031 when <i>al-Andalus</i> broke up into a number of mostly independent mini-states and principalities called <i><a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">taifas</a></i>. In 1013, invading Berbers <a href="/wiki/Sacks_of_C%C3%B3rdoba_(1009%E2%80%9313)" title="Sacks of Córdoba (1009–13)">sacked</a> Córdoba, massacring its inhabitants, pillaging the city, and burning the palace complex to the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> After 1031, the <i>taifas</i> were generally too weak to defend themselves against repeated raids and demands for tribute from the Christian states to the north and west, which were known to the Muslims as "the Galician nations",<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and which had spread from their initial strongholds in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Galicia" title="Kingdom of Galicia">Galicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">Asturias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cantabria" title="Cantabria">Cantabria</a>, the Basque country, and the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Marca_Hispanica" title="Marca Hispanica">Marca Hispanica</a></i> to become the Kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Navarre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">León</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aragon" title="Kingdom of Aragon">Aragon</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/County_of_Barcelona" title="County of Barcelona">County of Barcelona</a>. Eventually raids turned into conquests, and in response the <i>Taifa</i> kings were forced to request help from the <a href="/wiki/Almoravid" class="mw-redirect" title="Almoravid">Almoravids</a>, Muslim Berber rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>. Their desperate maneuver would eventually fall to their disadvantage, however, as the Almoravids they had summoned from the south went on to conquer and annex all the <i>Taifa</i> kingdoms.
</p>
<h3><span id="Almoravids.2C_Almohads.2C_and_Marinids"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Almoravids,_Almohads,_and_Marinids">Almoravids, Almohads, and Marinids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Almoravids, Almohads, and Marinids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Muslim_presence_in_the_Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Timeline of the Muslim presence in the Iberian peninsula">Timeline of the Muslim presence in the Iberian peninsula</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Empire_almoravide.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Empire_almoravide.PNG/170px-Empire_almoravide.PNG" decoding="async" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Empire_almoravide.PNG/255px-Empire_almoravide.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Empire_almoravide.PNG/340px-Empire_almoravide.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1220" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Empire_almoravide.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the extent of the <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravid empire</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Almohad_Expansion.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Almohad_Expansion.png/170px-Almohad_Expansion.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Almohad_Expansion.png/255px-Almohad_Expansion.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Almohad_Expansion.png/340px-Almohad_Expansion.png 2x" data-file-width="678" data-file-height="407" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Almohad_Expansion.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Expansion of the Almohad state in the 12th century</div></div></div>
<p>In 1086 the <a href="/wiki/Almoravids" class="mw-redirect" title="Almoravids">Almoravid</a> ruler of Morocco, <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_ibn_Tashfin" title="Yusuf ibn Tashfin">Yusuf ibn Tashfin</a>, was invited by the Muslim princes in Iberia to defend them against <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_VI_of_Castile" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfonso VI of Castile">Alfonso VI</a>, King of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">León</a>. In that year, Tashfin crossed the straits to <a href="/wiki/Algeciras" title="Algeciras">Algeciras</a> and inflicted a severe defeat on the Christians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sagrajas" title="Battle of Sagrajas">Battle of Sagrajas</a>. By 1094, ibn Tashfin had removed all Muslim princes in Iberia and had annexed their states, except for the one at <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza" title="Zaragoza">Zaragoza</a>. He also regained <a href="/wiki/Valencia,_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Valencia, Spain">Valencia</a> from the Christians.
</p><p>The Almoravids were succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Almohad" class="mw-redirect" title="Almohad">Almohads</a>, another Berber dynasty, after the victory of <a href="/wiki/Yaqub,_Almohad_Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Yaqub, Almohad Caliph">Abu Yusuf Ya'qub al-Mansur</a> over the Castilian <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_VIII_of_Castile" title="Alfonso VIII of Castile">Alfonso VIII</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Alarcos" title="Battle of Alarcos">Battle of Alarcos</a> in 1195. In 1212, a coalition of Christian kings under the leadership of the Castilian Alfonso VIII defeated the Almohads at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Las_Navas_de_Tolosa" title="Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa">Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa</a>. The Almohads continued to rule Al-Andalus for another decade, though with much reduced power and prestige. The civil wars following the death of <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_II,_Almohad_Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Yusuf II, Almohad Caliph">Abu Ya'qub Yusuf II</a> rapidly led to the re-establishment of taifas. The taifas, newly independent but now weakened, were quickly conquered by Portugal, Castile, and Aragon. After the fall of <a href="/wiki/Murcia" title="Murcia">Murcia</a> (1243) and the <a href="/wiki/Algarve" title="Algarve">Algarve</a> (1249), only the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">Emirate of Granada</a> survived as a Muslim state, and only as a tributary of Castile until 1492. Most of its tribute was paid in gold that was carried to Iberia from present-day <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a> through the merchant routes of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>.
</p><p>The last Muslim threat to the Christian kingdoms was the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Marinids" class="mw-redirect" title="Marinids">Marinids</a> in Morocco during the 14th century. They took Granada into their sphere of influence and occupied some of its cities, like <a href="/wiki/Algeciras" title="Algeciras">Algeciras</a>. However, they were unable to take <a href="/wiki/Tarifa" title="Tarifa">Tarifa</a>, which held out until the arrival of the Castilian Army led by <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_XI" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfonso XI">Alfonso XI</a>. The Castilian king, with the help of <a href="/wiki/Afonso_IV_of_Portugal" title="Afonso IV of Portugal">Afonso IV of Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_IV_of_Aragon" title="Peter IV of Aragon">Peter IV of Aragon</a>, decisively defeated the Marinids at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_R%C3%ADo_Salado" title="Battle of Río Salado">Battle of Río Salado</a> in 1340 and took Algeciras in 1344. <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a>, then under Granadian rule, was <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Siege_of_Gibraltar" title="Fifth Siege of Gibraltar">besieged in 1349–50</a>. Alfonso XI and most of his army perished by the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>. His successor, <a href="/wiki/Peter_of_Castile" title="Peter of Castile">Peter of Castile</a>, made peace with the Muslims and turned his attention to Christian lands, starting a period of almost 150 years of rebellions and wars between the Christian states that secured the survival of Granada.
</p>
<h3><span id="Emirate_of_Granada.2C_its_fall.2C_and_aftermath"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Emirate_of_Granada,_its_fall,_and_aftermath">Emirate of Granada, its fall, and aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Emirate of Granada, its fall, and aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">Emirate of Granada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nasrid_dynasty" title="Nasrid dynasty">Nasrid dynasty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Granada_War" title="Granada War">Granada War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:El_rey_chico_de_Granada.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/El_rey_chico_de_Granada.jpg/170px-El_rey_chico_de_Granada.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/El_rey_chico_de_Granada.jpg/255px-El_rey_chico_de_Granada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/El_rey_chico_de_Granada.jpg/340px-El_rey_chico_de_Granada.jpg 2x" data-file-width="463" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:El_rey_chico_de_Granada.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting of Muhammad XII, last sultan of Granada. Date of this painting and its current location are unknown.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nasrid_Dynasty_Textile_Fragment.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Nasrid_Dynasty_Textile_Fragment.jpg/170px-Nasrid_Dynasty_Textile_Fragment.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="91" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Nasrid_Dynasty_Textile_Fragment.jpg/255px-Nasrid_Dynasty_Textile_Fragment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Nasrid_Dynasty_Textile_Fragment.jpg/340px-Nasrid_Dynasty_Textile_Fragment.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="642" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nasrid_Dynasty_Textile_Fragment.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A silk textile fragment from the last Muslim dynasty of Al-Andalus, the <a href="/wiki/Nasrid_dynasty" title="Nasrid dynasty">Nasrid Dynasty</a> (1232 - 1492), with the epigraphic inscription "glory to our lord the Sultan". <sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>From the mid 13th to the late 15th century, the only remaining domain of al-Andalus was the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">Emirate of Granada</a>, the last Muslim stronghold in the Iberian Peninsula. The emirate was established by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_I_of_Granada" title="Muhammad I of Granada">Muhammad ibn al-Ahmar</a> in 1230 and was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Nasrid_dynasty" title="Nasrid dynasty">Nasrid dynasty</a>, the longest reigning dynasty in the history of al-Andalus. Although surrounded by Castilian lands, the emirate was wealthy through being tightly integrated in Mediterranean trade networks and enjoyed a period of considerable cultural and economic prosperity.<sup id="cite_ref-Page_120_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Page_120-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> However, for most of its existence Granada was a tributary state, with Nasrid emirs paying tribute to Castilian kings. Granada's status as a tributary state and its favorable geographic location, with the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(Spain)" title="Sierra Nevada (Spain)">Sierra Nevada</a> as a natural barrier, helped to prolong Nasrid rule and allowed the emirate to prosper as a regional <a href="/wiki/Entrep%C3%B4t" title="Entrepôt">entrepôt</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> and the rest of Africa. The city of Granada also served as a refuge for Muslims fleeing during the <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a>, accepting numerous Muslims expelled from Christian controlled areas, doubling the size of the city<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and even becoming one of the largest in Europe throughout the 15th century in terms of population.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google152_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google152-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salida_de_la_familia_de_Boabdil_de_la_Alhambra.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Salida_de_la_familia_de_Boabdil_de_la_Alhambra.jpg/220px-Salida_de_la_familia_de_Boabdil_de_la_Alhambra.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Salida_de_la_familia_de_Boabdil_de_la_Alhambra.jpg/330px-Salida_de_la_familia_de_Boabdil_de_la_Alhambra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Salida_de_la_familia_de_Boabdil_de_la_Alhambra.jpg/440px-Salida_de_la_familia_de_Boabdil_de_la_Alhambra.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="830" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salida_de_la_familia_de_Boabdil_de_la_Alhambra.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muhammad XII's family in the <a href="/wiki/Alhambra" title="Alhambra">Alhambra</a> moments after the fall of Granada, by Manuel Gómez-Moreno González, c. 1880</div></div></div>
<p>In 1469, the marriage of <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">Ferdinand of Aragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella of Castile</a> signaled the launch of the final assault on the emirate. The King and Queen convinced Pope <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_IV" title="Pope Sixtus IV">Sixtus IV</a> to declare their war a crusade. The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a> crushed one center of resistance after another until finally on January 2, 1492, after a long siege, the emirate's last sultan <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_XII_of_Granada" title="Muhammad XII of Granada">Muhammad XII</a> surrendered the city and the fortress palace, the renowned <a href="/wiki/Alhambra" title="Alhambra">Alhambra</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Granada" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of Granada">Fall of Granada</a>).
</p><p>By this time Muslims in <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Castile</a> numbered half a million. After the fall, "100,000 had died or been enslaved, 200,000 emigrated, and 200,000 remained as the residual population. Many of the Muslim elite, including <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_XII_of_Granada" title="Muhammad XII of Granada">Muhammad XII</a>, who had been given the area of the <a href="/wiki/Alpujarras" title="Alpujarras">Alpujarras</a> mountains as a principality, found life under Christian rule intolerable and passed over into North Africa."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Under the conditions of the Capitulations of 1492, the Muslims in Granada were to be allowed to continue to practice their religion.
</p><p>Mass <a href="/wiki/Forced_conversions_of_Muslims_in_Spain" title="Forced conversions of Muslims in Spain">forced conversions</a> of Muslims in 1499 led to a <a href="/wiki/Rebellion_of_the_Alpujarras_(1499%E2%80%931501)" title="Rebellion of the Alpujarras (1499–1501)">revolt</a> that spread to Alpujarras and the mountains of <a href="/wiki/Ronda" title="Ronda">Ronda</a>; after this uprising the capitulations were revoked.<sup id="cite_ref-Mediano2013_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mediano2013-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In 1502 the Catholic Monarchs decreed the forced conversion of all Muslims living under the rule of the Crown of Castile,<sup id="cite_ref-Majid2004_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Majid2004-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> although in the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">Aragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valencia" title="Valencia">Valencia</a> (both now part of Spain) the open practice of Islam was allowed until 1526.<sup id="cite_ref-Grieve2009_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grieve2009-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Descendants of the Muslims were subject to expulsions from Spain between 1609 and 1614 (see <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_of_the_Moriscos" title="Expulsion of the Moriscos">Expulsion of the Moriscos</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
The last mass prosecution against <a href="/wiki/Morisco" title="Morisco">Moriscos</a> for <a href="/wiki/Crypto-Islam" title="Crypto-Islam">crypto-Islamic</a> practices occurred in Granada in 1727, with most of those convicted receiving relatively light sentences. From then on, indigenous Islam is considered to have been extinguished in Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geschichte_des_Kost%C3%BCms_(1905)_(14784104832).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Geschichte_des_Kost%C3%BCms_%281905%29_%2814784104832%29.jpg/220px-Geschichte_des_Kost%C3%BCms_%281905%29_%2814784104832%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Geschichte_des_Kost%C3%BCms_%281905%29_%2814784104832%29.jpg/330px-Geschichte_des_Kost%C3%BCms_%281905%29_%2814784104832%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Geschichte_des_Kost%C3%BCms_%281905%29_%2814784104832%29.jpg/440px-Geschichte_des_Kost%C3%BCms_%281905%29_%2814784104832%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2096" data-file-height="2384" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geschichte_des_Kost%C3%BCms_(1905)_(14784104832).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clothing of al-Andalus in the 15th century, during the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">Emirate of Granada</a></div></div></div>
<p>The society of al-Andalus was made up of three main religious groups: Muslims, Christians, and Jews. The Muslims, although united on the religious level, had several ethnic divisions, the main being the distinction between the Arabs and the <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a>. The Arab elite regarded non-Arab Muslims as second-class citizens; and they were particularly scornful of the Berbers.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ethnic structure of al-Andalus consisted of Arabs at the top of the social scale followed by, in descending order, Berbers, <a href="/wiki/Muladies" class="mw-redirect" title="Muladies">Muladies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozarabes" class="mw-redirect" title="Mozarabes">Mozarabes</a>, and Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Each of these communities inhabited distinct neighborhoods in the cities. In the 10th century a massive conversion of Christians took place, and <i><a href="/wiki/Muladi" title="Muladi">muladies</a></i> (Muslims of native <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian</a> origin), formed the majority of Muslims. The Muladies had spoken in a <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance</a> dialect of Latin called <a href="/wiki/Mozarabic_language" title="Mozarabic language">Mozarabic</a> while increasingly adopting the Arabic language, which eventually evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Andalusi_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Andalusi Arabic">Andalusi Arabic</a> in which Muslims, Jews, and Christians became monolingual in the last surviving Muslim state in the Iberian Peninsula, the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">Emirate of Granada</a> (1230–1492). Eventually, the Muladies, and later the Berber tribes, adopted an Arabic identity like the majority of subject people in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>. Muladies, together with other Muslims, comprised eighty percent of the population of al-Andalus by 1100.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlick1999Chapter_5:_Ethnic_Relations_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlick1999Chapter_5:_Ethnic_Relations-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mozarab" class="mw-redirect" title="Mozarab">Mozarabs</a> were Christians who had long lived under Muslim and Arab rule, adopting many Arab customs, art, and words, while still maintaining their Christian and Latin rituals and their own Romance languages.
</p><p>The Jewish population worked mainly as tax collectors, in <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a>, or as doctors or ambassadors. At the end of the 15th century there were about 50,000 <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> in Granada and roughly 100,000 in the whole of Islamic Iberia.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><br />
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Muslims_under_the_Caliphate">Non-Muslims under the Caliphate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Non-Muslims under the Caliphate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/La_Convivencia" title="La Convivencia">La Convivencia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_Jewish_culture_in_the_Iberian_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden age of Jewish culture in the Iberian Peninsula">Golden age of Jewish culture in the Iberian Peninsula</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChristianAndMuslimPlayingChess-cropped2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/ChristianAndMuslimPlayingChess-cropped2.jpg/220px-ChristianAndMuslimPlayingChess-cropped2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/ChristianAndMuslimPlayingChess-cropped2.jpg/330px-ChristianAndMuslimPlayingChess-cropped2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/ChristianAndMuslimPlayingChess-cropped2.jpg/440px-ChristianAndMuslimPlayingChess-cropped2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="361" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChristianAndMuslimPlayingChess-cropped2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Christian and a Muslim playing chess in 13th-century al-Andalus</div></div></div>
<p>Non-Muslims were given the status of <i><a href="/wiki/Dhimmi" title="Dhimmi">ahl al-dhimma</a> </i>(the people under protection), with adult men paying a "<a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">Jizya</a>" tax, equal to one dinar per year with exemptions for the elderly and the disabled. Those who were neither Christians nor Jews, such as pagans, were given the status of <i><a href="/wiki/Majus" title="Majus">Majus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The treatment of non-Muslims in the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> has been a subject of considerable debate among scholars and commentators, especially those interested in drawing parallels to the coexistence of Muslims and non-Muslims in the modern world.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andalus_cantor.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Andalus_cantor.JPG/220px-Andalus_cantor.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Andalus_cantor.JPG/330px-Andalus_cantor.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Andalus_cantor.JPG/440px-Andalus_cantor.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1944" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andalus_cantor.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of a Jewish <a href="/wiki/Hazzan" title="Hazzan">cantor</a> reading the <a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">Passover</a> story in al-Andalus, from a 14th-century Spanish <a href="/wiki/Haggadah" title="Haggadah">Haggadah</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Spain" title="History of the Jews in Spain">Jews</a> constituted more than five percent of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Al-Andalus was a key centre of Jewish life during the early <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, producing important scholars and one of the most stable and wealthy Jewish communities.
</p><p>The longest period of relative tolerance began after 912 with the reign of <a href="/wiki/Abd-ar-Rahman_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Abd-ar-Rahman III">Abd-ar-Rahman III</a> and his son, <a href="/wiki/Al-Hakam_II" title="Al-Hakam II">Al-Hakam II</a>, when the Jews of al-Andalus prospered, devoting themselves to the service of the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Caliphate of Córdoba">Caliphate of Córdoba</a>, to the study of the sciences, and to commerce and industry, especially trading in <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slaves</a>, in this way promoting the prosperity of the country. Southern Iberia became an asylum for the oppressed Jews of other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Almoravids" class="mw-redirect" title="Almoravids">Almoravids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Almohads" class="mw-redirect" title="Almohads">Almohads</a> there may have been intermittent persecution of Jews,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> but sources are extremely scarce and do not give a clear picture, though the situation appears to have deteriorated after 1160.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Muslim pogroms against Jews in al-Andalus occurred in Córdoba (1011) and in <a href="/wiki/1066_Granada_massacre" title="1066 Granada massacre">Granada</a> (1066).<sup id="cite_ref-Schweitzer267-268_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer267-268-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> However, massacres of <a href="/wiki/Dhimmi" title="Dhimmi"><i>dhimmis</i></a> are rare in Islamic history.<sup id="cite_ref-BL45_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BL45-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Almohad" class="mw-redirect" title="Almohad">Almohads</a>, who had taken control of the Almoravids' Maghribi and Andalusi territories by 1147,<sup id="cite_ref-islamicworldeb_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-islamicworldeb-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> far surpassed the <a href="/wiki/Almoravides" class="mw-redirect" title="Almoravides">Almoravides</a> in fundamentalist outlook, and they treated the non-Muslims harshly. Faced with the choice of either death or conversion, many Jews and Christians emigrated.<sup id="cite_ref-frank_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frank-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Some, such as the family of <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>, fled east to more tolerant Muslim lands.<sup id="cite_ref-frank_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frank-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2017</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Many ethnicities, religions, and races coexisted in al-Andalus, each contributing to its intellectual prosperity. Literacy in Islamic Iberia was far more widespread than in many other nations in the West at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Thus, it also had an important literary activity; one specialist of Al-Andalus' intellectual history, Maria Luisa Avila, says that <i>"biographical dictionaries have recorded information about thousands of distinguished people in every period from al-Andalus, who were </i>cultivators of knowledge<i>, particularly in the legal-religious sciences as well as authors"</i>, and that <i>"the exact number of scholars which appears in the biographical sources has not been established yet, but it surely exceeds six thousand."</i> <sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In the 11th century the <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a> (base 10) reached Europe, via Al-Andalus through Spanish <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslims</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>, together with knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and instruments like the <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">astrolabe</a>, first imported by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sylvester_II" title="Pope Sylvester II">Gerbert of Aurillac</a>. For this reason, the numerals came to be known in Europe as <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a>.
</p><p>From the earliest days, the Umayyads wanted to be seen as intellectual rivals to the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasids</a>, and for Córdoba to have libraries and educational institutions to rival <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad's</a>. Although there was a clear rivalry between the two powers, there was freedom to travel between the two caliphates,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> which helped spread new ideas and innovations over time.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Andalus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Moorish_architecture" title="Moorish architecture">Moorish architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mud%C3%A9jar" title="Mudéjar">Mudéjar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moorish_Revival_architecture" title="Moorish Revival architecture">Moorish Revival architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Granada%27s_sunset.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Granada%27s_sunset.jpg/220px-Granada%27s_sunset.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Granada%27s_sunset.jpg/330px-Granada%27s_sunset.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Granada%27s_sunset.jpg/440px-Granada%27s_sunset.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Granada%27s_sunset.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Alhambra" title="Alhambra">Alhambra</a>, constructed by the orders of the first Nasrid emir <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_I_of_Granada" title="Muhammad I of Granada">Ibn al-Ahmar</a> in the 13th century</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CSM_185_(187).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/CSM_185_%28187%29.jpg/220px-CSM_185_%28187%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/CSM_185_%28187%29.jpg/330px-CSM_185_%28187%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/CSM_185_%28187%29.jpg/440px-CSM_185_%28187%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="882" data-file-height="810" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CSM_185_(187).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_I_of_Granada" title="Muhammad I of Granada">Muhammad I of Granada</a> (red shield), depicted in an illustration taken from the <i><a href="/wiki/Cantigas_de_Santa_Maria" title="Cantigas de Santa Maria">Cantigas de Santa María</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Alhambra" title="Alhambra">Alhambra</a> palace and fortress as well as the <a href="/wiki/Generalife" title="Generalife">Generalife</a> in Granada reflect the culture and art of the last centuries of Moorish rule of Al-Andalus.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The complex was completed at this stage towards the end of the rule by <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Yusuf I">Yusuf I</a> (1333–1353) and <a href="/wiki/Muhammed_V,_Sultan_of_Granada" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammed V, Sultan of Granada">Muhammed V, Sultan of Granada</a> (1353–1391). Artists and intellectuals took refuge at Alhambra after the Reconquista began to roll back Muslim territory. The site integrates natural qualities with constructed structures and gardens, and is a testament to Moorish culture in Al-Andalus and to the skills of the Muslim artisans, craftsmen, and builders of their era.
</p><p>The decoration within the palace comes from the last great period of Al-Andalus art in Granada, with little of the Byzantine influence of contemporary <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_architecture" title="Abbasid architecture">Abbasid architecture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Artists endlessly reproduced the same forms and trends, creating a new style that developed over the course of the Nasrid Dynasty using elements created and developed during the centuries of Muslim rule on the Peninsula, including the Caliphate <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_arch" title="Horseshoe arch">horseshoe arch</a>, the Almohad sebka (a grid of <a href="/wiki/Rhombuses" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhombuses">rhombuses</a>), the Almoravid palm, and unique combinations of these, as well as innovations such as stilted arches and <a href="/wiki/Muqarnas" title="Muqarnas">muqarnas</a> (stalactite ceiling decorations). Columns and muqarnas appear in several chambers, and the interiors of numerous palaces are decorated with <a href="/wiki/Arabesques" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabesques">arabesques</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">calligraphy</a>.
